      <p>
       The
       <b>
        speed of light
       </b>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">
        vacuum
       </a>
       , commonly denoted
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         <b>
          c
         </b>
        </i>
       </span>
       , is a universal
       <a href="/wiki/Physical_constant" title="Physical constant">
        physical constant
       </a>
       important in many areas of
       <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">
        physics
       </a>
       . Its exact value is
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008299792458000000♠
        </span>
        299,792,458
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metres_per_second" title="Metres per second">
         metres per second
        </a>
       </span>
       (approximately
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008300000000000000♠
        </span>
        3.00
        <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ×
        </span>
        10
        <sup>
         8
        </sup>
        m/s
       </span>
       , or 300,000 km/s (186,000 mi/s)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imperial_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-imperial-4">
         [Note 3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ). It is exact because the unit of length, the
       <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">
        metre
       </a>
       , is defined from this constant and the
       <a href="/wiki/Second#International_second" title="Second">
        international standard for time
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-penrose_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-penrose-5">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to
       <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">
        special relativity
       </a>
       ,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       is the maximum speed at which all conventional matter and hence all known forms of
       <a href="/wiki/Physical_information" title="Physical information">
        information
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">
        universe
       </a>
       can travel. Though this speed is most commonly associated with
       <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">
        light
       </a>
       , it is in fact the speed at which all
       <a href="/wiki/Massless_particle" title="Massless particle">
        massless particles
       </a>
       and changes of the associated
       <a href="/wiki/Field_(physics)" title="Field (physics)">
        fields
       </a>
       travel in vacuum (including
       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">
        electromagnetic radiation
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_wave" title="Gravitational wave">
        gravitational waves
       </a>
       ). Such particles and waves travel at
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       regardless of the motion of the source or the
       <a href="/wiki/Inertial_frame_of_reference" title="Inertial frame of reference">
        inertial reference frame
       </a>
       of the observer. In the
       <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_relativity" title="Theory of relativity">
        theory of relativity
       </a>
       ,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       interrelates
       <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">
        space and time
       </a>
       , and also appears in the famous equation of
       <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass–energy equivalence">
        mass–energy equivalence
       </a>
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         E
        </i>
        =
        <i>
         mc
        </i>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeClerq_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LeClerq-6">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The speed at which light propagates through
       <a href="/wiki/Transparency_and_translucency" title="Transparency and translucency">
        transparent materials
       </a>
       , such as glass or air, is less than
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       ; similarly, the speed of electromagnetic waves in wire cables is slower than
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       . The ratio between
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       and the speed
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         v
        </i>
       </span>
       at which light travels in a material is called the
       <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">
        refractive index
       </a>
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         n
        </i>
       </span>
       of the material (
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         n
        </i>
        =
        <i>
         c
        </i>
        /
        <i>
         v
        </i>
       </span>
       ). For example, for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">
        visible light
       </a>
       the refractive index of glass is typically around 1.5, meaning that light in glass travels at
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="texhtml">
         <i>
          c
         </i>
        </span>
        / 1.5 ≈ 200,000 km/s (124,000 mi/s)
       </span>
       ; the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refractive_index_of_air" title="Refractive index of air">
        refractive index of air
       </a>
       for visible light is about 1.0003, so the speed of light in air is about 299,700 km/s (186,220 mi/s) (about 90 km/s (56 mi/s) slower than
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       ).
      </p>
      <p>
       For many practical purposes, light and other electromagnetic waves will appear to propagate instantaneously, but for long distances and very sensitive measurements, their finite speed has noticeable effects. In communicating with distant
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_probes" title="Space probes">
        space probes
       </a>
       , it can take minutes to hours for a message to get from Earth to the spacecraft, or vice versa. The light seen from stars left them many years ago, allowing the study of the history of the universe by looking at distant objects. The finite speed of light also limits the theoretical maximum speed of
       <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">
        computers
       </a>
       , since information must be sent within the computer from chip to chip. The speed of light can be used with
       <a href="/wiki/Time_of_flight" title="Time of flight">
        time of flight
       </a>
       measurements to measure large distances to high precision.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Ole_R%C3%B8mer" title="Ole Rømer">
        Ole Rømer
       </a>
       first
       <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B8mer%27s_determination_of_the_speed_of_light" title="Rømer's determination of the speed of light">
        demonstrated in 1676
       </a>
       that light travels at a finite speed (as opposed to instantaneously) by studying the apparent motion of
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       's moon
       <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">
        Io
       </a>
       . In 1865,
       <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">
        James Clerk Maxwell
       </a>
       proposed that light was an electromagnetic wave, and therefore travelled at the speed
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       appearing in his theory of electromagnetism.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1905,
       <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">
        Albert Einstein
       </a>
       postulated that the speed of light
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       with respect to any inertial frame is a constant and is independent of the motion of the light source.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stachel_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-stachel-8">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He explored the consequences of that postulate by deriving the theory of relativity and in doing so showed that the parameter
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       had relevance outside of the context of light and electromagnetism.
      </p>
      <p>
       After centuries of increasingly precise measurements, in 1975 the speed of light was known to be
       <span style="white-space: nowrap">
        299
        <span style="margin-left: 0.25em">
         792
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left: 0.25em">
         458
        </span>
       </span>
       m/s (
       <span style="white-space: nowrap">
        983
        <span style="margin-left: 0.25em">
         571
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left: 0.25em">
         056
        </span>
       </span>
       ft/s;
       <span style="white-space: nowrap">
        186
        <span style="margin-left: 0.25em">
         282.397
        </span>
       </span>
       mi/s) with a
       <a href="/wiki/Measurement_uncertainty" title="Measurement uncertainty">
        measurement uncertainty
       </a>
       of 4 parts per billion. In 1983, the
       <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">
        metre
       </a>
       was redefined in the
       <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">
        International System of Units
       </a>
       (SI) as the distance travelled by light in vacuum in 1/
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008299792458000000♠
        </span>
        299
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         792
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         458
        </span>
       </span>
       of a
       <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">
        second
       </a>
       . As a result, the numerical value of
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       in metres per second is now fixed exactly by the definition of the metre.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIPM_SI_units_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-BIPM_SI_units-9">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span id="Numerical_value.2C_notation.2C_and_units">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Numerical_value,_notation,_and_units">
        Numerical value, notation, and units
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The speed of light in vacuum is usually denoted by a lowercase
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       , for "constant" or the Latin
       <i>
        <span lang="la" xml:lang="la">
         <i>
          <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/celeritas" title="wikt:celeritas">
           celeritas
          </a>
         </i>
        </span>
       </i>
       (meaning "swiftness, celerity"). In 1856,
       <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Eduard_Weber" title="Wilhelm Eduard Weber">
        Wilhelm Eduard Weber
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Kohlrausch" title="Rudolf Kohlrausch">
        Rudolf Kohlrausch
       </a>
       had used
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       for a different constant later shown to equal
       <span class="nowrap">
        √
        <span style="border-top:1px solid; padding:0 0.1em;">
         2
        </span>
       </span>
       times the speed of light in vacuum. Historically, the symbol
       <i>
        V
       </i>
       was used as an alternative symbol for the speed of light, introduced by
       <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">
        James Clerk Maxwell
       </a>
       in 1865. In 1894,
       <a href="/wiki/Paul_Drude" title="Paul Drude">
        Paul Drude
       </a>
       redefined
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       with its modern meaning.
       <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">
        Einstein
       </a>
       used
       <i>
        V
       </i>
       in his
       <a href="/wiki/Annus_Mirabilis_papers" title="Annus Mirabilis papers">
        original German-language papers
       </a>
       on special relativity in 1905, but in 1907 he switched to
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       , which by then had become the standard symbol for the speed of light.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yc_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Yc-10">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Sometimes
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       is used for the speed of waves in
       <i>
        any
       </i>
       material medium, and
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       <sub>
        0
       </sub>
       for the speed of light in vacuum.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-handbook_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-handbook-12">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This subscripted notation, which is endorsed in official SI literature,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIPM_SI_units_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-BIPM_SI_units-9">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       has the same form as other related constants: namely,
       <i>
        μ
       </i>
       <sub>
        0
       </sub>
       for the
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_permeability" title="Vacuum permeability">
        vacuum permeability
       </a>
       or magnetic constant,
       <i>
        ε
       </i>
       <sub>
        0
       </sub>
       for the
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_permittivity" title="Vacuum permittivity">
        vacuum permittivity
       </a>
       or electric constant, and
       <i>
        Z
       </i>
       <sub>
        0
       </sub>
       for the
       <a href="/wiki/Impedance_of_free_space" title="Impedance of free space">
        impedance of free space
       </a>
       . This article uses
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       exclusively for the speed of light in vacuum.
      </p>
      <p>
       Since 1983, the metre has been defined in the
       <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">
        International System of Units
       </a>
       (SI) as the distance light travels in vacuum in ​
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         <span class="nowrap">
          <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
           7008299792458000000♠
          </span>
          299
          <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
           792
          </span>
          <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
           458
          </span>
         </span>
        </sub>
       </span>
       of a second. This definition fixes the speed of light in vacuum at exactly
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008299792458000000♠
        </span>
        299,792,458 m/s
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boyes_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Boyes-13">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fundamental_Physical_Constants_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Fundamental_Physical_Constants-14">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jespersen_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jespersen-15">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As a
       <a href="/wiki/Physical_constant#Dimensional_and_dimensionless_physical_constants" title="Physical constant">
        dimensional physical constant
       </a>
       , the numerical value of
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       is different for different unit systems.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-imperial_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-imperial-4">
         [Note 3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In branches of physics in which
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       appears often, such as in relativity, it is common to use systems of
       <a href="/wiki/Natural_units" title="Natural units">
        natural units
       </a>
       of measurement or the
       <a href="/wiki/Geometrized_unit_system" title="Geometrized unit system">
        geometrized unit system
       </a>
       where
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="texhtml">
         <i>
          c
         </i>
        </span>
        = 1
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lawrie_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lawrie-17">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hsu1_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hsu1-18">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Using these units,
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       does not appear explicitly because multiplication or division by 1 does not affect the result.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Fundamental_role_in_physics">
        Fundamental role in physics
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">
        Special relativity
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/One-way_speed_of_light" title="One-way speed of light">
        One-way speed of light
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The speed at which light waves propagate in vacuum is independent both of the motion of the wave source and of the
       <a href="/wiki/Inertial_frame_of_reference" title="Inertial frame of reference">
        inertial frame of reference
       </a>
       of the observer.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [Note 4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This invariance of the speed of light was postulated by Einstein in 1905,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stachel_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-stachel-8">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       after being motivated by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_theory_of_electromagnetism" title="Maxwell's theory of electromagnetism">
        Maxwell's theory of electromagnetism
       </a>
       and the lack of evidence for the
       <a href="/wiki/Luminiferous_aether" title="Luminiferous aether">
        luminiferous aether
       </a>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       it has since been consistently confirmed by many experiments. It is only possible to verify experimentally that the two-way speed of light (for example, from a source to a mirror and back again) is frame-independent, because it is impossible to measure the
       <a href="/wiki/One-way_speed_of_light" title="One-way speed of light">
        one-way speed of light
       </a>
       (for example, from a source to a distant detector) without some convention as to how clocks at the source and at the detector should be synchronized. However, by adopting
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Einstein_synchronization" title="Einstein synchronization">
        Einstein synchronization
       </a>
       for the clocks, the one-way speed of light becomes equal to the two-way speed of light by definition.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hsu2_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hsu2-21">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhang_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Zhang-22">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_theory_of_relativity" title="Special theory of relativity">
        special theory of relativity
       </a>
       explores the consequences of this invariance of
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       with the assumption that the laws of physics are the same in all inertial frames of reference.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One consequence is that
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       is the speed at which all
       <a href="/wiki/Massless_particle" title="Massless particle">
        massless particles
       </a>
       and waves, including light, must travel in vacuum.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lorentz_factor.svg">
         <img alt="γ starts at 1 when v equals zero and stays nearly constant for small v's, then it sharply curves upwards and has a vertical asymptote, diverging to positive infinity as v approaches c." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1118" data-file-width="1102" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Lorentz_factor.svg/170px-Lorentz_factor.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Lorentz_factor.svg/255px-Lorentz_factor.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Lorentz_factor.svg/340px-Lorentz_factor.svg.png 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lorentz_factor.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Lorentz factor
         <i>
          γ
         </i>
         as a function of velocity. It starts at 1 and approaches infinity as
         <i>
          v
         </i>
         approaches
         <i>
          c
         </i>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Special relativity has many counterintuitive and experimentally verified implications.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These include the
       <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass–energy equivalence">
        equivalence of mass and energy
       </a>
       <span class="nowrap">
        (
        <i>
         E
        </i>
        =
        <i>
         mc
        </i>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        )
       </span>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Length_contraction" title="Length contraction">
        length contraction
       </a>
       (moving objects shorten),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [Note 5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Time_dilation" title="Time dilation">
        time dilation
       </a>
       (moving clocks run more slowly). The factor
       <i>
        γ
       </i>
       by which lengths contract and times dilate is known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_factor" title="Lorentz factor">
        Lorentz factor
       </a>
       and is given by
       <span class="nowrap">
        <i>
         γ
        </i>
        = (1 −
        <i>
         v
        </i>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        /
        <i>
         c
        </i>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
        )
        <sup>
         −1/2
        </sup>
       </span>
       , where
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       is the speed of the object. The difference of
       <i>
        γ
       </i>
       from 1 is negligible for speeds much slower than
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       , such as most everyday speeds—in which case special relativity is closely approximated by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_relativity" title="Galilean relativity">
        Galilean relativity
       </a>
       —but it increases at relativistic speeds and diverges to infinity as
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       approaches
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       . For example, a time dilation factor of
       <i>
        γ
       </i>
       = 2 occurs at a relative velocity of 86.6% of the speed of light (
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       = .866
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       ). Similarly, a time dilation factor of
       <i>
        γ
       </i>
       = 10 occurs at
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       = 99.5%
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The results of special relativity can be summarized by treating space and time as a unified structure known as
       <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">
        spacetime
       </a>
       (with
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       relating the units of space and time), and requiring that physical theories satisfy a special
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symmetry_in_physics" title="Symmetry in physics">
        symmetry
       </a>
       called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorentz_invariance" title="Lorentz invariance">
        Lorentz invariance
       </a>
       , whose mathematical formulation contains the parameter
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Lorentz invariance is an almost universal assumption for modern physical theories, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">
        quantum electrodynamics
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_chromodynamics" title="Quantum chromodynamics">
        quantum chromodynamics
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">
        Standard Model
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">
        particle physics
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">
        general relativity
       </a>
       . As such, the parameter
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       is ubiquitous in modern physics, appearing in many contexts that are unrelated to light. For example, general relativity predicts that
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       is also the
       <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_gravity" title="Speed of gravity">
        speed of gravity
       </a>
       and of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_waves" title="Gravitational waves">
        gravitational waves
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartle_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hartle-30">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [Note 6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-inertial_frame" title="Non-inertial frame">
        non-inertial frames
       </a>
       of reference (gravitationally curved spacetime or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accelerated_reference_frame" title="Accelerated reference frame">
        accelerated reference frames
       </a>
       ), the
       <i>
        local
       </i>
       speed of light is constant and equal to
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       , but the
       <a href="/wiki/Propagation_of_light_in_non-inertial_reference_frames" title="Propagation of light in non-inertial reference frames">
        speed of light along a trajectory of finite length
       </a>
       can differ from
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       , depending on how distances and times are defined.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbs1997_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gibbs1997-33">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It is generally assumed that fundamental constants such as
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       have the same value throughout spacetime, meaning that they do not depend on location and do not vary with time. However, it has been suggested in various theories that the
       <a href="/wiki/Variable_speed_of_light" title="Variable speed of light">
        speed of light may have changed over time
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ellis_Uzan_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Ellis_Uzan-34">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mota_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mota-35">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       No conclusive evidence for such changes has been found, but they remain the subject of ongoing research.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uzan_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Uzan-36">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Camelia_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Camelia-37">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It also is generally assumed that the speed of light is
       <a href="/wiki/Isotropy" title="Isotropy">
        isotropic
       </a>
       , meaning that it has the same value regardless of the direction in which it is measured. Observations of the emissions from nuclear
       <a href="/wiki/Energy_level" title="Energy level">
        energy levels
       </a>
       as a function of the orientation of the emitting
       <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">
        nuclei
       </a>
       in a magnetic field (see
       <a href="/wiki/Hughes%E2%80%93Drever_experiment" title="Hughes–Drever experiment">
        Hughes–Drever experiment
       </a>
       ), and of rotating
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_resonator" title="Optical resonator">
        optical resonators
       </a>
       (see
       <a href="/wiki/Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment#Recent_experiments" title="Michelson–Morley experiment">
        Resonator experiments
       </a>
       ) have put stringent limits on the possible two-way anisotropy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herrmann_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Herrmann-38">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lang_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lang-39">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Upper_limit_on_speeds">
        Upper limit on speeds
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       According to special relativity, the energy of an object with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">
        rest mass
       </a>
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       and speed
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       is given by
       <span class="nowrap">
        <i>
         γmc
        </i>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
       </span>
       , where
       <i>
        γ
       </i>
       is the Lorentz factor defined above. When
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       is zero,
       <i>
        γ
       </i>
       is equal to one, giving rise to the famous
       <span class="nowrap">
        <i>
         E
        </i>
        =
        <i>
         mc
        </i>
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
       </span>
       formula for
       <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass–energy equivalence">
        mass–energy equivalence
       </a>
       . The
       <i>
        γ
       </i>
       factor approaches infinity as
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       approaches
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       , and it would take an infinite amount of energy to accelerate an object with mass to the speed of light. The speed of light is the upper limit for the speeds of objects with positive rest mass, and individual photons cannot travel faster than the speed of light.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is experimentally established in many
       <a href="/wiki/Tests_of_relativistic_energy_and_momentum" title="Tests of relativistic energy and momentum">
        tests of relativistic energy and momentum
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Relativity_of_Simultaneity.svg">
         <img alt="Three pairs of coordinate axes are depicted with the same origin A; in the green frame, the x axis is horizontal and the ct axis is vertical; in the red frame, the x′ axis is slightly skewed upwards, and the ct′ axis slightly skewed rightwards, relative to the green axes; in the blue frame, the x′′ axis is somewhat skewed downwards, and the ct′′ axis somewhat skewed leftwards, relative to the green axes. A point B on the green x axis, to the left of A, has zero ct, positive ct′, and negative ct′′." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="563" data-file-width="563" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Relativity_of_Simultaneity.svg/220px-Relativity_of_Simultaneity.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Relativity_of_Simultaneity.svg/330px-Relativity_of_Simultaneity.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Relativity_of_Simultaneity.svg/440px-Relativity_of_Simultaneity.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Relativity_of_Simultaneity.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Event A precedes B in the red frame, is simultaneous with B in the green frame, and follows B in the blue frame.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       More generally, it is normally impossible for information or energy to travel faster than
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       . One argument for this follows from the counter-intuitive implication of special relativity known as the
       <a href="/wiki/Relativity_of_simultaneity" title="Relativity of simultaneity">
        relativity of simultaneity
       </a>
       . If the spatial distance between two events A and B is greater than the time interval between them multiplied by
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       then there are frames of reference in which A precedes B, others in which B precedes A, and others in which they are simultaneous. As a result, if something were travelling faster than
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       relative to an inertial frame of reference, it would be travelling backwards in time relative to another frame, and
       <a href="/wiki/Causality_(physics)" title="Causality (physics)">
        causality
       </a>
       would be violated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [Note 7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_p74_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Taylor_p74-46">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In such a frame of reference, an "effect" could be observed before its "cause". Such a violation of causality has never been recorded,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhang_22-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Zhang-22">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and would lead to
       <a href="/wiki/Paradox" title="Paradox">
        paradoxes
       </a>
       such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Tachyonic_antitelephone" title="Tachyonic antitelephone">
        tachyonic antitelephone
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Faster-than-light_observations_and_experiments">
        Faster-than-light observations and experiments
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Faster-than-light" title="Faster-than-light">
        Faster-than-light
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Superluminal_motion" title="Superluminal motion">
        Superluminal motion
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       There are situations in which it may seem that matter, energy, or information travels at speeds greater than
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       , but they do not. For example, as is discussed in the
       <a href="#In_a_medium">
        propagation of light in a medium
       </a>
       section below, many wave velocities can exceed
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       . For example, the
       <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">
        phase velocity
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">
        X-rays
       </a>
       through most glasses can routinely exceed
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but phase velocity does not determine the velocity at which waves convey information.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       If a laser beam is swept quickly across a distant object, the spot of light can move faster than
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       , although the initial movement of the spot is delayed because of the time it takes light to get to the distant object at the speed
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       . However, the only physical entities that are moving are the laser and its emitted light, which travels at the speed
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       from the laser to the various positions of the spot. Similarly, a shadow projected onto a distant object can be made to move faster than
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       , after a delay in time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In neither case does any matter, energy, or information travel faster than light.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbs_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gibbs-51">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The rate of change in the distance between two objects in a frame of reference with respect to which both are moving (their
       <a href="/wiki/Faster-than-light#Closing_speeds" title="Faster-than-light">
        closing speed
       </a>
       ) may have a value in excess of
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       . However, this does not represent the speed of any single object as measured in a single inertial frame.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbs_51-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gibbs-51">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Certain quantum effects appear to be transmitted instantaneously and therefore faster than
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       , as in the
       <a href="/wiki/EPR_paradox" title="EPR paradox">
        EPR paradox
       </a>
       . An example involves the
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_state" title="Quantum state">
        quantum states
       </a>
       of two particles that can be
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_entanglement" title="Quantum entanglement">
        entangled
       </a>
       . Until either of the particles is observed, they exist in a
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_superposition" title="Quantum superposition">
        superposition
       </a>
       of two quantum states. If the particles are separated and one particle's quantum state is observed, the other particle's quantum state is determined instantaneously (i.e., faster than light could travel from one particle to the other). However, it is impossible to control which quantum state the first particle will take on when it is observed, so information cannot be transmitted in this manner.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbs_51-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gibbs-51">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Another quantum effect that predicts the occurrence of faster-than-light speeds is called the
       <a href="/wiki/Hartman_effect" title="Hartman effect">
        Hartman effect
       </a>
       : under certain conditions the time needed for a
       <a href="/wiki/Virtual_particle" title="Virtual particle">
        virtual particle
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_tunnelling" title="Quantum tunnelling">
        tunnel
       </a>
       through a barrier is constant, regardless of the thickness of the barrier.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muga_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Muga-53">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Recami_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Recami-54">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This could result in a virtual particle crossing a large gap faster-than-light. However, no information can be sent using this effect.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wynne_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Wynne-55">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       So-called
       <a href="/wiki/Superluminal_motion" title="Superluminal motion">
        superluminal motion
       </a>
       is seen in certain astronomical objects,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       such as the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_jet" title="Relativistic jet">
        relativistic jets
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Radio_galaxy" title="Radio galaxy">
        radio galaxies
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">
        quasars
       </a>
       . However, these jets are not moving at speeds in excess of the speed of light: the apparent superluminal motion is a
       <a href="/wiki/Graphical_projection" title="Graphical projection">
        projection
       </a>
       effect caused by objects moving near the speed of light and approaching Earth at a small angle to the line of sight: since the light which was emitted when the jet was farther away took longer to reach the Earth, the time between two successive observations corresponds to a longer time between the instants at which the light rays were emitted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In models of the expanding universe, the farther galaxies are from each other, the faster they drift apart. This receding is not due to motion
       <i>
        through
       </i>
       space, but rather to the
       <a href="/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space" title="Metric expansion of space">
        expansion of space
       </a>
       itself.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbs_51-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gibbs-51">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For example, galaxies far away from Earth appear to be moving away from the Earth with a speed proportional to their distances. Beyond a boundary called the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_sphere" title="Hubble sphere">
        Hubble sphere
       </a>
       , the rate at which their distance from Earth increases becomes greater than the speed of light.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harrison_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Harrison-58">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Propagation_of_light">
        Propagation of light
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Classical_physics" title="Classical physics">
        classical physics
       </a>
       , light is described as a type of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_wave" title="Electromagnetic wave">
        electromagnetic wave
       </a>
       . The classical behaviour of the
       <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_field" title="Electromagnetic field">
        electromagnetic field
       </a>
       is described by
       <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell's equations">
        Maxwell's equations
       </a>
       , which predict that the speed
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       with which electromagnetic waves (such as light) propagate through the vacuum is related to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_constant" title="Electric constant">
        electric constant
       </a>
       <i>
        ε
       </i>
       <sub>
        0
       </sub>
       and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_constant" title="Magnetic constant">
        magnetic constant
       </a>
       <i>
        μ
       </i>
       <sub>
        0
       </sub>
       by the equation
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle c={\frac {1}{\sqrt {\varepsilon _{0}\mu _{0}}}}\ .}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               c
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mn>
                 1
                </mn>
                <msqrt>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   ε
                   <!-- ε -->
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    0
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   μ
                   <!-- μ -->
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    0
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                </msqrt>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mtext>
              </mtext>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle c={\frac {1}{\sqrt {\varepsilon _{0}\mu _{0}}}}\ .}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\displaystyle c={\frac {1}{\sqrt {\varepsilon _{0}\mu _{0}}}}\ .}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f83cb831ce59c5745ee4e7af60b4f68710405b6b" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:12.698ex; height:6.176ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       In modern
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_physics" title="Quantum physics">
        quantum physics
       </a>
       , the electromagnetic field is described by the theory of
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics" title="Quantum electrodynamics">
        quantum electrodynamics
       </a>
       (QED). In this theory, light is described by the fundamental excitations (or quanta) of the electromagnetic field, called
       <a href="/wiki/Photon" title="Photon">
        photons
       </a>
       . In QED, photons are
       <a href="/wiki/Massless_particle" title="Massless particle">
        massless particles
       </a>
       and thus, according to special relativity, they travel at the speed of light in vacuum.
      </p>
      <p>
       Extensions of QED in which the photon has a mass have been considered. In such a theory, its speed would depend on its frequency, and the invariant speed
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       of special relativity would then be the upper limit of the speed of light in vacuum.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gibbs1997_33-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Gibbs1997-33">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       No variation of the speed of light with frequency has been observed in rigorous testing,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaefer_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Schaefer-60">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sakharov_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sakharov-61">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Füllekrug_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Füllekrug-62">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       putting stringent limits on the mass of the photon. The limit obtained depends on the model used: if the massive photon is described by
       <a href="/wiki/Proca_action" title="Proca action">
        Proca theory
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adelberger_63-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-adelberger-63">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the experimental upper bound for its mass is about 10
       <sup>
        −57
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">
        grams
       </a>
       ;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sidharth_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sidharth-64">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       if photon mass is generated by a
       <a href="/wiki/Higgs_mechanism" title="Higgs mechanism">
        Higgs mechanism
       </a>
       , the experimental upper limit is less sharp,
       <span class="nowrap">
        <i>
         m
        </i>
        ≤ 10
        <sup>
         −14
        </sup>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electron_volt#As_a_unit_of_mass" title="Electron volt">
         eV/c
         <sup>
          2
         </sup>
        </a>
       </span>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adelberger_63-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-adelberger-63">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (roughly 2 × 10
       <sup>
        −47
       </sup>
       g).
      </p>
      <p>
       Another reason for the speed of light to vary with its frequency would be the failure of special relativity to apply to arbitrarily small scales, as predicted by some proposed theories of
       <a href="/wiki/Quantum_gravity" title="Quantum gravity">
        quantum gravity
       </a>
       . In 2009, the observation of the spectrum of
       <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst" title="Gamma-ray burst">
        gamma-ray burst
       </a>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=GRB_090510&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="GRB 090510 (page does not exist)">
        GRB 090510
       </a>
       did not find any difference in the speeds of photons of different energies, confirming that Lorentz invariance is verified at least down to the scale of the
       <a href="/wiki/Planck_length" title="Planck length">
        Planck length
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        l
       </i>
       <sub>
        P
       </sub>
       =
       <span class="nowrap">
        √
        <span style="border-top:1px solid; padding:0 0.1em;">
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reduced_Planck_constant" title="Reduced Planck constant">
          <i>
           ħ
          </i>
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">
          <i>
           G
          </i>
         </a>
         /
         <i>
          c
         </i>
         <sup>
          3
         </sup>
        </span>
       </span>
       ≈
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         6965161630000000000♠
        </span>
        1.6163
        <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ×
        </span>
        10
        <sup>
         −35
        </sup>
        m
       </span>
       ) divided by 1.2.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="In_a_medium">
        In a medium
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">
        Refractive index
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In a medium, light usually does not propagate at a speed equal to
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       ; further, different types of light wave will travel at different speeds. The speed at which the individual crests and troughs of a
       <a href="/wiki/Plane_wave" title="Plane wave">
        plane wave
       </a>
       (a wave filling the whole space, with only one
       <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">
        frequency
       </a>
       ) propagate is called the
       <a href="/wiki/Phase_velocity" title="Phase velocity">
        phase velocity
       </a>
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        p
       </sub>
       . An actual physical signal with a finite extent (a pulse of light) travels at a different speed. The largest part of the pulse travels at the
       <a href="/wiki/Group_velocity" title="Group velocity">
        group velocity
       </a>
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        g
       </sub>
       , and its earliest part travels at the
       <a href="/wiki/Front_velocity" title="Front velocity">
        front velocity
       </a>
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        f
       </sub>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frontgroupphase.gif">
         <img alt="A modulated wave moves from left to right. There are three points marked with a dot: A blue dot at a node of the carrier wave, a green dot at the maximum of the envelope, and a red dot at the front of the envelope." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="272" data-file-width="440" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Frontgroupphase.gif/220px-Frontgroupphase.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Frontgroupphase.gif/330px-Frontgroupphase.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/27/Frontgroupphase.gif 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frontgroupphase.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The blue dot moves at the speed of the ripples, the phase velocity; the green dot moves with the speed of the envelope, the group velocity; and the red dot moves with the speed of the foremost part of the pulse, the front velocity
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The phase velocity is important in determining how a light wave travels through a material or from one material to another. It is often represented in terms of a
       <i>
        refractive index
       </i>
       . The refractive index of a material is defined as the ratio of
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       to the phase velocity
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        p
       </sub>
       in the material: larger indices of refraction indicate lower speeds. The refractive index of a material may depend on the light's frequency, intensity,
       <a href="/wiki/Polarization_(waves)" title="Polarization (waves)">
        polarization
       </a>
       , or direction of propagation; in many cases, though, it can be treated as a material-dependent constant. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refractive_index_of_air" title="Refractive index of air">
        refractive index of air
       </a>
       is approximately 1.0003.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podesta_66-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Podesta-66">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Denser media, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Optical_properties_of_water_and_ice" title="Optical properties of water and ice">
        water
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">
        glass
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Material_properties_of_diamond#Optical_properties" title="Material properties of diamond">
        diamond
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       have refractive indexes of around 1.3, 1.5 and 2.4, respectively, for visible light. In exotic materials like
       <a href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_condensate" title="Bose–Einstein condensate">
        Bose–Einstein condensates
       </a>
       near absolute zero, the effective speed of light may be only a few metres per second. However, this represents absorption and re-radiation delay between atoms, as do all slower-than-
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       speeds in material substances. As an extreme example of light "slowing" in matter, two independent teams of physicists claimed to bring light to a "complete standstill" by passing it through a Bose–Einstein condensate of the element
       <a href="/wiki/Rubidium" title="Rubidium">
        rubidium
       </a>
       , one team at
       <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">
        Harvard University
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Rowland_Institute_for_Science" title="Rowland Institute for Science">
        Rowland Institute for Science
       </a>
       in Cambridge, Mass., and the other at the
       <a href="/wiki/Harvard%E2%80%93Smithsonian_Center_for_Astrophysics" title="Harvard–Smithsonian Center for Astrophysics">
        Harvard–Smithsonian Center for Astrophysics
       </a>
       , also in Cambridge. However, the popular description of light being "stopped" in these experiments refers only to light being stored in the excited states of atoms, then re-emitted at an arbitrarily later time, as stimulated by a second laser pulse. During the time it had "stopped," it had ceased to be light. This type of behaviour is generally microscopically true of all transparent media which "slow" the speed of light.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In transparent materials, the refractive index generally is greater than 1, meaning that the phase velocity is less than
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       . In other materials, it is possible for the refractive index to become smaller than 1 for some frequencies; in some exotic materials it is even possible for the index of refraction to become negative.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The requirement that causality is not violated implies that the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Real_and_imaginary_parts" title="Real and imaginary parts">
        real and imaginary parts
       </a>
       of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dielectric_constant" title="Dielectric constant">
        dielectric constant
       </a>
       of any material, corresponding respectively to the index of refraction and to the
       <a href="/wiki/Attenuation_coefficient" title="Attenuation coefficient">
        attenuation coefficient
       </a>
       , are linked by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kramers%E2%80%93Kronig_relation" title="Kramers–Kronig relation">
        Kramers–Kronig relations
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In practical terms, this means that in a material with refractive index less than 1, the absorption of the wave is so quick that no signal can be sent faster than
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       A pulse with different group and phase velocities (which occurs if the phase velocity is not the same for all the frequencies of the pulse) smears out over time, a process known as
       <a href="/wiki/Dispersion_(optics)" title="Dispersion (optics)">
        dispersion
       </a>
       . Certain materials have an exceptionally low (or even zero) group velocity for light waves, a phenomenon called
       <a href="/wiki/Slow_light" title="Slow light">
        slow light
       </a>
       , which has been confirmed in various experiments.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The opposite, group velocities exceeding
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       , has also been shown in experiment.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It should even be possible for the group velocity to become infinite or negative, with pulses travelling instantaneously or backwards in time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MilonniCh2_78-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MilonniCh2-78">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       None of these options, however, allow information to be transmitted faster than
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       . It is impossible to transmit information with a light pulse any faster than the speed of the earliest part of the pulse (the
       <a href="/wiki/Front_velocity" title="Front velocity">
        front velocity
       </a>
       ). It can be shown that this is (under certain assumptions) always equal to
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MilonniCh2_78-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-MilonniCh2-78">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <p>
       It is possible for a particle to travel through a medium faster than the phase velocity of light in that medium (but still slower than
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       ). When a
       <a href="/wiki/Charged_particle" title="Charged particle">
        charged particle
       </a>
       does that in a
       <a href="/wiki/Dielectric" title="Dielectric">
        dielectric
       </a>
       material, the electromagnetic equivalent of a
       <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">
        shock wave
       </a>
       , known as
       <a href="/wiki/Cherenkov_radiation" title="Cherenkov radiation">
        Cherenkov radiation
       </a>
       , is emitted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Practical_effects_of_finiteness">
        Practical effects of finiteness
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The speed of light is of relevance to
       <a href="/wiki/Telecommunication" title="Telecommunication">
        communications
       </a>
       : the one-way and
       <a href="/wiki/Round-trip_delay_time" title="Round-trip delay time">
        round-trip delay time
       </a>
       are greater than zero. This applies from small to astronomical scales. On the other hand, some techniques depend on the finite speed of light, for example in distance measurements.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Small_scales">
        Small scales
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In
       <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer" title="Supercomputer">
        supercomputers
       </a>
       , the speed of light imposes a limit on how quickly data can be sent between
       <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">
        processors
       </a>
       . If a processor operates at 1
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gigahertz" title="Gigahertz">
        gigahertz
       </a>
       , a signal can only travel a maximum of about 30 centimetres (1 ft) in a single cycle. Processors must therefore be placed close to each other to minimize communication latencies; this can cause difficulty with cooling. If clock frequencies continue to increase, the speed of light will eventually become a limiting factor for the internal design of single
       <a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">
        chips
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-processorlimit_80-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-processorlimit-80">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-processorlimit2_81-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-processorlimit2-81">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Large_distances_on_Earth">
        Large distances on Earth
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Given that the equatorial circumference of the Earth is about
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7007400750000000000♠
        </span>
        40
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         075
        </span>
        km
       </span>
       and that
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       is about
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008300000000000000♠
        </span>
        300
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         000
        </span>
        km/s
       </span>
       , the theoretical shortest time for a piece of information to travel half the globe along the surface is about 67 milliseconds. When light is travelling around the globe in an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_fibre" title="Optical fibre">
        optical fibre
       </a>
       , the actual transit time is longer, in part because the speed of light is slower by about 35% in an optical fibre, depending on its refractive index
       <i>
        n
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [Note 8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Furthermore, straight lines rarely occur in global communications situations, and delays are created when the signal passes through an electronic switch or signal regenerator.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spaceflights_and_astronomy">
        Spaceflights and astronomy
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Speed_of_light_from_Earth_to_Moon.gif">
         <img alt="The diameter of the moon is about one quarter of that of Earth, and their distance is about thirty times the diameter of Earth. A beam of light starts from the Earth and reaches the Moon in about a second and a quarter." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="92" data-file-width="1024" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Speed_of_light_from_Earth_to_Moon.gif/220px-Speed_of_light_from_Earth_to_Moon.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Speed_of_light_from_Earth_to_Moon.gif/330px-Speed_of_light_from_Earth_to_Moon.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Speed_of_light_from_Earth_to_Moon.gif/440px-Speed_of_light_from_Earth_to_Moon.gif 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Speed_of_light_from_Earth_to_Moon.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A beam of light is depicted travelling between the Earth and the Moon in the time it takes a light pulse to move between them: 1.255 seconds at their mean orbital (surface-to-surface) distance. The relative sizes and separation of the Earth–Moon system are shown to scale.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Similarly, communications between the Earth and spacecraft are not instantaneous. There is a brief delay from the source to the receiver, which becomes more noticeable as distances increase. This delay was significant for communications between
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mission_Control_Center" title="Mission Control Center">
        ground control
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_8" title="Apollo 8">
        Apollo 8
       </a>
       when it became the first manned spacecraft to orbit the Moon: for every question, the ground control station had to wait at least three seconds for the answer to arrive.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The communications delay between Earth and
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Mars
       </a>
       can vary between five and twenty minutes depending upon the relative positions of the two planets. As a consequence of this, if a robot on the surface of Mars were to encounter a problem, its human controllers would not be aware of it until at least five minutes later, and possibly up to twenty minutes later; it would then take a further five to twenty minutes for instructions to travel from Earth to Mars.
      </p>
      <p>
       NASA must wait several hours for information from a probe orbiting Jupiter, and if it needs to correct a navigation error, the fix will not arrive at the spacecraft for an equal amount of time, creating a risk of the correction not arriving in time.
      </p>
      <p>
       Receiving light and other signals from distant astronomical sources can even take much longer. For example, it has taken 13 billion (13
       <span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">
        ×
       </span>
       10
       <sup>
        <span style="white-space:nowrap">
         9
        </span>
       </sup>
       ) years for light to travel to Earth from the faraway galaxies viewed in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hubble_Ultra_Deep_Field" title="Hubble Ultra Deep Field">
        Hubble Ultra Deep Field
       </a>
       images.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubble_86-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hubble-86">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Those photographs, taken today, capture images of the galaxies as they appeared 13 billion years ago, when the universe was less than a billion years old.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hubble_86-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hubble-86">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The fact that more distant objects appear to be younger, due to the finite speed of light, allows astronomers to infer the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolution_of_stars" title="Evolution of stars">
        evolution of stars
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_formation_and_evolution" title="Galaxy formation and evolution">
        of galaxies
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_universe" title="History of the universe">
        of the universe
       </a>
       itself.
      </p>
      <p>
       Astronomical distances are sometimes expressed in
       <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">
        light-years
       </a>
       , especially in
       <a href="/wiki/Popular_science" title="Popular science">
        popular science
       </a>
       publications and media.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A light-year is the distance light travels in one year, around 9461 billion kilometres, 5879 billion miles, or 0.3066
       <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">
        parsecs
       </a>
       . In round figures, a light year is nearly 10 trillion kilometres or nearly 6 trillion miles.
       <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">
        Proxima Centauri
       </a>
       , the closest star to Earth after the Sun, is around 4.2 light-years away.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-starchild_89-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-starchild-89">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Distance_measurement">
        Distance measurement
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distance_measurement" title="Distance measurement">
        Distance measurement
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">
        Radar
       </a>
       systems measure the distance to a target by the time it takes a radio-wave pulse to return to the radar antenna after being reflected by the target: the distance to the target is half the round-trip
       <a href="/wiki/Radar#Transit_time" title="Radar">
        transit time
       </a>
       multiplied by the speed of light. A
       <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">
        Global Positioning System
       </a>
       (GPS) receiver measures its distance to GPS satellites based on how long it takes for a radio signal to arrive from each satellite, and from these distances calculates the receiver's position. Because light travels about
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7005300000000000000♠
        </span>
        300
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         000
        </span>
        kilometres
       </span>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008299337984000000♠
        </span>
        186
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         000
        </span>
        mi
       </span>
       ) in one second, these measurements of small fractions of a second must be very precise. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_Laser_Ranging_Experiment" title="Lunar Laser Ranging Experiment">
        Lunar Laser Ranging Experiment
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Radar_astronomy" title="Radar astronomy">
        radar astronomy
       </a>
       and the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Space_Network" title="Deep Space Network">
        Deep Space Network
       </a>
       determine distances to the Moon,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science265_5171_482_90-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-science265_5171_482-90">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       planets
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cm26_181_91-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cm26_181-91">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and spacecraft,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pieee95_11_2202_92-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pieee95_11_2202-92">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       respectively, by measuring round-trip transit times.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="High-frequency_trading">
        High-frequency trading
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The speed of light has become important in
       <a href="/wiki/High-frequency_trading" title="High-frequency trading">
        high-frequency trading
       </a>
       , where traders seek to gain minute advantages by delivering their trades to exchanges fractions of a second ahead of other traders. For example, traders have been switching to
       <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">
        microwave
       </a>
       communications between trading hubs, because of the advantage which microwaves travelling at near to the speed of light in air, have over
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fibre_optic" title="Fibre optic">
        fibre optic
       </a>
       signals which travel 30–40% slower at the speed of light through glass.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">
        Measurement
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       There are different ways to determine the value of
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       . One way is to measure the actual speed at which light waves propagate, which can be done in various astronomical and earth-based setups. However, it is also possible to determine
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       from other physical laws where it appears, for example, by determining the values of the electromagnetic constants
       <a href="/wiki/Relative_permittivity" title="Relative permittivity">
        <i>
         ε
        </i>
        <sub>
         0
        </sub>
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Permeability_(electromagnetism)" title="Permeability (electromagnetism)">
        <i>
         μ
        </i>
        <sub>
         0
        </sub>
       </a>
       and using their relation to
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       . Historically, the most accurate results have been obtained by separately determining the frequency and wavelength of a light beam, with their product equalling
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
