       In 1983 the metre was defined as "the length of the path travelled by light in vacuum during a time interval of ​
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         <span class="nowrap">
          <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
           7008299792458000000♠
          </span>
          299
          <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
           792
          </span>
          <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
           458
          </span>
         </span>
        </sub>
       </span>
       of a second",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Resolution_1_94-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Resolution_1-94">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       fixing the value of the speed of light at
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008299792458000000♠
        </span>
        299
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         792
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         458
        </span>
        m/s
       </span>
       by definition, as
       <a href="#Increased_accuracy_of_c_and_redefinition_of_the_metre_and_second">
        described below
       </a>
       . Consequently, accurate measurements of the speed of light yield an accurate realization of the metre rather than an accurate value of
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomical_measurements">
        Astronomical measurements
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Io_eclipse_speed_of_light_measurement.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="512" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Io_eclipse_speed_of_light_measurement.svg/330px-Io_eclipse_speed_of_light_measurement.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Io_eclipse_speed_of_light_measurement.svg/495px-Io_eclipse_speed_of_light_measurement.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Io_eclipse_speed_of_light_measurement.svg/660px-Io_eclipse_speed_of_light_measurement.svg.png 2x" width="330"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Io_eclipse_speed_of_light_measurement.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Measurement of the speed of light using the eclipse of Io by Jupiter
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">
        Outer space
       </a>
       is a convenient setting for measuring the speed of light because of its large scale and nearly perfect
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">
        vacuum
       </a>
       . Typically, one measures the time needed for light to traverse some reference distance in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_system" title="Solar system">
        solar system
       </a>
       , such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Radius" title="Radius">
        radius
       </a>
       of the Earth's orbit. Historically, such measurements could be made fairly accurately, compared to how accurately the length of the reference distance is known in Earth-based units. It is customary to express the results in
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">
        astronomical units
       </a>
       (AU) per day.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ole_Christensen_R%C3%B8mer" title="Ole Christensen Rømer">
        Ole Christensen Rømer
       </a>
       used an astronomical measurement to make
       <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B8mer%27s_determination_of_the_speed_of_light" title="Rømer's determination of the speed of light">
        the first quantitative estimate of the speed of light
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cohen_95-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cohen-95">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roemer_96-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-roemer-96">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       When measured from Earth, the periods of moons orbiting a distant planet are shorter when the Earth is approaching the planet than when the Earth is receding from it. The distance travelled by light from the planet (or its moon) to Earth is shorter when the Earth is at the point in its orbit that is closest to its planet than when the Earth is at the farthest point in its orbit, the difference in distance being the
       <a href="/wiki/Diameter" title="Diameter">
        diameter
       </a>
       of the Earth's orbit around the Sun. The observed change in the moon's orbital period is caused by the difference in the time it takes light to traverse the shorter or longer distance. Rømer observed this effect for
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       's innermost moon
       <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">
        Io
       </a>
       and deduced that light takes 22 minutes to cross the diameter of the Earth's orbit.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SoL_Aberration.svg">
         <img alt="A star emits a light ray which hits the objective of a telescope. While the light travels down the telescope to its eyepiece, the telescope moves to the right. For the light to stay inside the telescope, the telescope must be tilted to the right, causing the distant source to appear at a different location to the right." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="492" data-file-width="420" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/SoL_Aberration.svg/170px-SoL_Aberration.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/SoL_Aberration.svg/255px-SoL_Aberration.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/SoL_Aberration.svg/340px-SoL_Aberration.svg.png 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SoL_Aberration.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Aberration of light: light from a distant source appears to be from a different location for a moving telescope due to the finite speed of light.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Another method is to use the
       <a href="/wiki/Aberration_of_light" title="Aberration of light">
        aberration of light
       </a>
       , discovered and explained by
       <a href="/wiki/James_Bradley" title="James Bradley">
        James Bradley
       </a>
       in the 18th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradley1729_97-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bradley1729-97">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This effect results from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vector_addition" title="Vector addition">
        vector addition
       </a>
       of the velocity of light arriving from a distant source (such as a star) and the velocity of its observer (see diagram on the right). A moving observer thus sees the light coming from a slightly different direction and consequently sees the source at a position shifted from its original position. Since the direction of the Earth's velocity changes continuously as the Earth orbits the Sun, this effect causes the apparent position of stars to move around. From the angular difference in the position of stars (maximally 20.5
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcsecond" title="Arcsecond">
        arcseconds
       </a>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       it is possible to express the speed of light in terms of the Earth's velocity around the Sun, which with the known length of a year can be converted to the time needed to travel from the Sun to the Earth. In 1729, Bradley used this method to derive that light travelled 10,210 times faster than the Earth in its orbit (the modern figure is 10,066 times faster) or, equivalently, that it would take light 8 minutes 12 seconds to travel from the Sun to the Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradley1729_97-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bradley1729-97">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomical_unit">
        Astronomical unit
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       An astronomical unit (AU) is approximately the average distance between the Earth and Sun. It was redefined in 2012 as exactly
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7011149597870700000♠
        </span>
        149
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         597
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         870
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         700
        </span>
        m
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AU_redef_99-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AU_redef-99">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Previously the AU was not based on the
       <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">
        International System of Units
       </a>
       but in terms of the gravitational force exerted by the Sun in the framework of classical mechanics.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
        <a href="#cite_note-102">
         [Note 9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The current definition uses the recommended value in metres for the previous definition of the astronomical unit, which was determined by measurement.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AU_redef_99-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-AU_redef-99">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This redefinition is analogous to that of the metre, and likewise has the effect of fixing the speed of light to an exact value in astronomical units per second (via the exact speed of light in metres per second).
      </p>
      <p>
       Previously, the inverse of
       <span class="texhtml">
        <i>
         c
        </i>
       </span>
       expressed in seconds per astronomical unit was measured by comparing the time for radio signals to reach different spacecraft in the Solar System, with their position calculated from the gravitational effects of the Sun and various planets. By combining many such measurements, a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Best_fit" title="Best fit">
        best fit
       </a>
       value for the light time per unit distance could be obtained. For example, in 2009, the best estimate, as approved by the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">
        International Astronomical Union
       </a>
       (IAU), was:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pitjeva09_103-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Pitjeva09-103">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU_104-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAU-104">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
        <a href="#cite_note-105">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        light time for unit distance: t
        <sub>
         au
        </sub>
        =
        <span class="nowrap">
         <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
          7002499004783836000♠
         </span>
         499.004
         <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
          783
         </span>
         <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
          836
         </span>
         (10) s
        </span>
       </dd>
       <dd>
        <i>
         c
        </i>
        =
        <span class="nowrap">
         <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
          7008299792457999999♠
         </span>
         0.002
         <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
          003
         </span>
         <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
          988
         </span>
         <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
          804
         </span>
         <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
          10
         </span>
         (4) AU/s
        </span>
        =
        <span class="nowrap">
         <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
          7002173144632674000♠
         </span>
         173.144
         <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
          632
         </span>
         <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
          674
         </span>
         (3) AU/day.
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The relative uncertainty in these measurements is 0.02 parts per billion (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         6989200000000000000♠
        </span>
        2
        <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ×
        </span>
        10
        <sup>
         −11
        </sup>
       </span>
       ), equivalent to the uncertainty in Earth-based measurements of length by interferometry.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
        <a href="#cite_note-106">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since the metre is defined to be the length travelled by light in a certain time interval, the measurement of the light time in terms of the previous definition of the astronomical unit can also be interpreted as measuring the length of an AU (old definition) in metres.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
        <a href="#cite_note-107">
         [Note 10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Time_of_flight_techniques">
        Time of flight techniques
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="center">
       <div class="thumb tnone">
        <div class="thumbinner" style="width:882px;">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Michelson_speed_of_light_measurement_1930.jpg">
          <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="470" data-file-width="1168" height="354" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Michelson_speed_of_light_measurement_1930.jpg/880px-Michelson_speed_of_light_measurement_1930.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e8/Michelson_speed_of_light_measurement_1930.jpg 1.5x" width="880"/>
         </a>
         <div class="thumbcaption">
          <div class="magnify">
           <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Michelson_speed_of_light_measurement_1930.jpg" title="Enlarge">
           </a>
          </div>
          One of the last and most accurate time of flight measurements, Michelson, Pease and Pearson's 1930–35 experiment used a rotating mirror and a one-mile (1.6 km) long vacuum chamber which the light beam traversed 10 times. It achieved accuracy of ±11 km/s
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fizeau.JPG">
         <img alt="A light ray passes horizontally through a half-mirror and a rotating cog wheel, is reflected back by a mirror, passes through the cog wheel, and is reflected by the half-mirror into a monocular." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="257" data-file-width="341" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Fizeau.JPG/220px-Fizeau.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Fizeau.JPG/330px-Fizeau.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4c/Fizeau.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fizeau.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Diagram of the
         <a href="/wiki/Fizeau%E2%80%93Foucault_apparatus" title="Fizeau–Foucault apparatus">
          Fizeau apparatus
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A method of measuring the speed of light is to measure the time needed for light to travel to a mirror at a known distance and back. This is the working principle behind the
       <a href="/wiki/Fizeau%E2%80%93Foucault_apparatus" title="Fizeau–Foucault apparatus">
        Fizeau–Foucault apparatus
       </a>
       developed by
       <a href="/wiki/Hippolyte_Fizeau" title="Hippolyte Fizeau">
        Hippolyte Fizeau
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Foucault" title="Léon Foucault">
        Léon Foucault
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The setup as used by Fizeau consists of a beam of light directed at a mirror 8 kilometres (5 mi) away. On the way from the source to the mirror, the beam passes through a rotating cogwheel. At a certain rate of rotation, the beam passes through one gap on the way out and another on the way back, but at slightly higher or lower rates, the beam strikes a tooth and does not pass through the wheel. Knowing the distance between the wheel and the mirror, the number of teeth on the wheel, and the rate of rotation, the speed of light can be calculated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-How_108-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-How-108">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The method of Foucault replaces the cogwheel by a rotating mirror. Because the mirror keeps rotating while the light travels to the distant mirror and back, the light is reflected from the rotating mirror at a different angle on its way out than it is on its way back. From this difference in angle, the known speed of rotation and the distance to the distant mirror the speed of light may be calculated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
        <a href="#cite_note-109">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Nowadays, using
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oscilloscopes" title="Oscilloscopes">
        oscilloscopes
       </a>
       with time resolutions of less than one nanosecond, the speed of light can be directly measured by timing the delay of a light pulse from a laser or an LED reflected from a mirror. This method is less precise (with errors of the order of 1%) than other modern techniques, but it is sometimes used as a laboratory experiment in college physics classes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
        <a href="#cite_note-110">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
        <a href="#cite_note-111">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
        <a href="#cite_note-112">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div style="clear:both;">
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnetic_constants">
        Electromagnetic constants
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       An option for deriving
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       that does not directly depend on a measurement of the propagation of electromagnetic waves is to use the relation between
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_permittivity" title="Vacuum permittivity">
        vacuum permittivity
       </a>
       <i>
        ε
       </i>
       <sub>
        0
       </sub>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_permeability" title="Vacuum permeability">
        vacuum permeability
       </a>
       <i>
        μ
       </i>
       <sub>
        0
       </sub>
       established by Maxwell's theory:
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       = 1/(
       <i>
        ε
       </i>
       <sub>
        0
       </sub>
       <i>
        μ
       </i>
       <sub>
        0
       </sub>
       ). The vacuum permittivity may be determined by measuring the
       <a href="/wiki/Capacitance" title="Capacitance">
        capacitance
       </a>
       and dimensions of a
       <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">
        capacitor
       </a>
       , whereas the value of the
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_permeability" title="Vacuum permeability">
        vacuum permeability
       </a>
       is fixed at exactly
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         6993400000000000000♠
        </span>
        4π
        <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ×
        </span>
        10
        <sup>
         −7
        </sup>
        H⋅m
        <sup>
         −1
        </sup>
       </span>
       through the definition of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ampere_(unit)" title="Ampere (unit)">
        ampere
       </a>
       . Rosa and Dorsey used this method in 1907 to find a value of
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008299710000000000♠
        </span>
        299
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         710
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ±
        </span>
        22 km/s
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Essen1948_113-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Essen1948-113">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RosaDorsey_114-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-RosaDorsey-114">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cavity_resonance">
        Cavity resonance
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waves_in_Box.svg">
         <img alt="A box with three waves in it; there are one and a half wavelength of the top wave, one of the middle one, and a half of the bottom one." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="324" data-file-width="350" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Waves_in_Box.svg/220px-Waves_in_Box.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Waves_in_Box.svg/330px-Waves_in_Box.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Waves_in_Box.svg/440px-Waves_in_Box.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waves_in_Box.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Electromagnetic
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standing_waves" title="Standing waves">
          standing waves
         </a>
         in a cavity.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Another way to measure the speed of light is to independently measure the frequency
       <i>
        f
       </i>
       and wavelength
       <i>
        λ
       </i>
       of an electromagnetic wave in vacuum. The value of
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       can then be found by using the relation
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        fλ
       </i>
       . One option is to measure the resonance frequency of a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cavity_resonator" title="Cavity resonator">
        cavity resonator
       </a>
       . If the dimensions of the resonance cavity are also known, these can be used to determine the wavelength of the wave. In 1946,
       <a href="/wiki/Louis_Essen" title="Louis Essen">
        Louis Essen
       </a>
       and A.C. Gordon-Smith established the frequency for a variety of
       <a href="/wiki/Normal_mode" title="Normal mode">
        normal modes
       </a>
       of microwaves of a
       <a href="/wiki/Microwave_cavity" title="Microwave cavity">
        microwave cavity
       </a>
       of precisely known dimensions. The dimensions were established to an accuracy of about ±0.8 μm using gauges calibrated by interferometry.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Essen1948_113-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Essen1948-113">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As the wavelength of the modes was known from the geometry of the cavity and from
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_theory" title="Electromagnetic theory">
        electromagnetic theory
       </a>
       , knowledge of the associated frequencies enabled a calculation of the speed of light.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Essen1948_113-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Essen1948-113">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
        <a href="#cite_note-115">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Essen–Gordon-Smith result,
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008299792000000000♠
        </span>
        299
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         792
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ±
        </span>
        9 km/s
       </span>
       , was substantially more precise than those found by optical techniques.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Essen1948_113-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Essen1948-113">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By 1950, repeated measurements by Essen established a result of
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008299792500000000♠
        </span>
        299
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         792
        </span>
        .5
        <span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ±
        </span>
        3.0 km/s
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Essen1950_116-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Essen1950-116">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A household demonstration of this technique is possible, using a
       <a href="/wiki/Microwave_oven" title="Microwave oven">
        microwave oven
       </a>
       and food such as marshmallows or margarine: if the turntable is removed so that the food does not move, it will cook the fastest at the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antinode" title="Antinode">
        antinodes
       </a>
       (the points at which the wave amplitude is the greatest), where it will begin to melt. The distance between two such spots is half the wavelength of the microwaves; by measuring this distance and multiplying the wavelength by the microwave frequency (usually displayed on the back of the oven, typically 2450 MHz), the value of
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       can be calculated, "often with less than 5% error".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
        <a href="#cite_note-117">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
        <a href="#cite_note-118">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Interferometry">
        Interferometry
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Interferometer_sol.svg">
         <img alt="Schematic of the working of a Michelson interferometer." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="190" data-file-width="345" height="121" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Interferometer_sol.svg/220px-Interferometer_sol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Interferometer_sol.svg/330px-Interferometer_sol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Interferometer_sol.svg/440px-Interferometer_sol.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Interferometer_sol.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         An interferometric determination of length. Left:
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constructive_interference" title="Constructive interference">
          constructive interference
         </a>
         ; Right:
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Destructive_interference" title="Destructive interference">
          destructive interference
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Interferometry" title="Interferometry">
        Interferometry
       </a>
       is another method to find the wavelength of electromagnetic radiation for determining the speed of light.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
        <a href="#cite_note-120">
         [Note 11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A
       <a href="/wiki/Coherence_(physics)" title="Coherence (physics)">
        coherent
       </a>
       beam of light (e.g. from a
       <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">
        laser
       </a>
       ), with a known frequency (
       <i>
        f
       </i>
       ), is split to follow two paths and then recombined. By adjusting the path length while observing the
       <a href="/wiki/Interference_(wave_propagation)" title="Interference (wave propagation)">
        interference pattern
       </a>
       and carefully measuring the change in path length, the wavelength of the light (
       <i>
        λ
       </i>
       ) can be determined. The speed of light is then calculated using the equation
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        λf
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Before the advent of laser technology, coherent
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiowave" title="Radiowave">
        radio
       </a>
       sources were used for interferometry measurements of the speed of light.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Froome1858_121-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Froome1858-121">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However interferometric determination of wavelength becomes less precise with wavelength and the experiments were thus limited in precision by the long wavelength (~0.4 cm (0.16 in)) of the radiowaves. The precision can be improved by using light with a shorter wavelength, but then it becomes difficult to directly measure the frequency of the light. One way around this problem is to start with a low frequency signal of which the frequency can be precisely measured, and from this signal progressively synthesize higher frequency signals whose frequency can then be linked to the original signal. A laser can then be locked to the frequency, and its wavelength can be determined using interferometry.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST_pub_122-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NIST_pub-122">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This technique was due to a group at the National Bureau of Standards (NBS) (which later became
       <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">
        NIST
       </a>
       ). They used it in 1972 to measure the speed of light in vacuum with a
       <a href="/wiki/Measurement_uncertainty" title="Measurement uncertainty">
        fractional uncertainty
       </a>
       of
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         6991350000000000000♠
        </span>
        3.5
        <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ×
        </span>
        10
        <sup>
         −9
        </sup>
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST_pub_122-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NIST_pub-122">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST_heterodyne_123-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NIST_heterodyne-123">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Essen1950_116-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-Essen1950-116">
           [106]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         1958
        </td>
        <td>
         K.D. Froome, radio interferometry
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="nowrap">
          <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
           7005299792500000000♠
          </span>
          299
          <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
           792
          </span>
          .50
          <span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">
           ±
          </span>
          0.10
         </span>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Froome1858_121-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-Froome1858-121">
           [110]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         1972
        </td>
        <td>
         Evenson
         <i>
          et al.
         </i>
         , laser interferometry
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="nowrap">
          <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
           7005299792456200000♠
          </span>
          299
          <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
           792
          </span>
          .4562
          <span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">
           ±
          </span>
          0.0011
         </span>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST_heterodyne_123-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-NIST_heterodyne-123">
           [112]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         1983
        </td>
        <td>
         17th CGPM, definition of the metre
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="nowrap">
          <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
           7005299792458000000♠
          </span>
          299
          <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
           792
          </span>
          .458
         </span>
         (exact)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Resolution_1_94-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-Resolution_1-94">
           [86]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       Until the
       <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">
        early modern period
       </a>
       , it was not known whether light travelled instantaneously or at a very fast finite speed. The first extant recorded examination of this subject was in
       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">
        ancient Greece
       </a>
       . The ancient Greeks, Muslim scholars, and classical European scientists long debated this until Rømer provided the first calculation of the speed of light. Einstein's Theory of Special Relativity concluded that the speed of light is constant regardless of one's frame of reference. Since then, scientists have provided increasingly accurate measurements.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">
        Early history
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Empedocles" title="Empedocles">
        Empedocles
       </a>
       (c. 490–430 BC) was the first to propose a theory of light
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
        <a href="#cite_note-131">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and claimed that light has a finite speed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
        <a href="#cite_note-132">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He maintained that light was something in motion, and therefore must take some time to travel.
       <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
        Aristotle
       </a>
       argued, to the contrary, that "light is due to the presence of something, but it is not a movement".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Statistics_133-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Statistics-133">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">
        Euclid
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
        Ptolemy
       </a>
       advanced Empedocles'
       <a href="/wiki/Emission_theory_(vision)" title="Emission theory (vision)">
        emission theory
       </a>
       of vision, where light is emitted from the eye, thus enabling sight. Based on that theory,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heron_of_Alexandria" title="Heron of Alexandria">
        Heron of Alexandria
       </a>
       argued that the speed of light must be
       <a href="/wiki/Infinity" title="Infinity">
        infinite
       </a>
       because distant objects such as stars appear immediately upon opening the eyes.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Early_Islamic_philosophy" title="Early Islamic philosophy">
        Early Islamic philosophers
       </a>
       initially agreed with the
       <a href="/wiki/Aristotelian_physics" title="Aristotelian physics">
        Aristotelian view
       </a>
       that light had no speed of travel. In 1021,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alhazen" title="Alhazen">
        Alhazen
       </a>
       (Ibn al-Haytham) published the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Optics" title="Book of Optics">
         Book of Optics
        </a>
       </i>
       , in which he presented a series of arguments dismissing the emission theory of
       <a href="/wiki/Visual_perception" title="Visual perception">
        vision
       </a>
       in favour of the now accepted intromission theory, in which light moves from an object into the eye.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
        <a href="#cite_note-134">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This led Alhazen to propose that light must have a finite speed,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Statistics_133-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Statistics-133">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamarneh_135-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hamarneh-135">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lester_136-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lester-136">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and that the speed of light is variable, decreasing in denser bodies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lester_136-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lester-136">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
        <a href="#cite_note-137">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He argued that light is substantial matter, the propagation of which requires time, even if this is hidden from our senses.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
        <a href="#cite_note-138">
         [126]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Also in the 11th century,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Rayh%C4%81n_al-B%C4%ABr%C5%ABn%C4%AB" title="Abū Rayhān al-Bīrūnī">
        Abū Rayhān al-Bīrūnī
       </a>
       agreed that light has a finite speed, and observed that the speed of light is much faster than the speed of sound.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
        <a href="#cite_note-139">
         [127]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the 13th century,
       <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">
        Roger Bacon
       </a>
       argued that the speed of light in air was not infinite, using philosophical arguments backed by the writing of Alhazen and Aristotle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindberg_140-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lindberg-140">
         [128]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
        <a href="#cite_note-141">
         [129]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 1270s,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Witelo" title="Witelo">
        Witelo
       </a>
       considered the possibility of light travelling at infinite speed in vacuum, but slowing down in denser bodies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall_142-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Marshall-142">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the early 17th century,
       <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">
        Johannes Kepler
       </a>
       believed that the speed of light was infinite, since empty space presents no obstacle to it.
       <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">
        René Descartes
       </a>
       argued that if the speed of light were to be finite, the Sun, Earth, and Moon would be noticeably out of alignment during a
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">
        lunar eclipse
       </a>
       . Since such misalignment had not been observed, Descartes concluded the speed of light was infinite. Descartes speculated that if the speed of light were found to be finite, his whole system of philosophy might be demolished.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Statistics_133-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Statistics-133">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Descartes' derivation of
       <a href="/wiki/Snell%27s_law" title="Snell's law">
        Snell's law
       </a>
       , he assumed that even though the speed of light was instantaneous, the denser the medium, the faster was light's speed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
        <a href="#cite_note-143">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat" title="Pierre de Fermat">
        Pierre de Fermat
       </a>
       derived Snell's law using the opposing assumption, the denser the medium the slower light traveled. Fermat also argued in support of a finite speed of light.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
        <a href="#cite_note-144">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="First_measurement_attempts">
        First measurement attempts
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 1629,
       <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Beeckman" title="Isaac Beeckman">
        Isaac Beeckman
       </a>
       proposed an experiment in which a person observes the flash of a cannon reflecting off a mirror about one mile (1.6 km) away. In 1638,
       <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">
        Galileo Galilei
       </a>
       proposed an experiment, with an apparent claim to having performed it some years earlier, to measure the speed of light by observing the delay between uncovering a lantern and its perception some distance away. He was unable to distinguish whether light travel was instantaneous or not, but concluded that if it were not, it must nevertheless be extraordinarily rapid.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2newsciences_124-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-2newsciences-124">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boyer_125-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-boyer-125">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1667, the
       <a href="/wiki/Accademia_del_Cimento" title="Accademia del Cimento">
        Accademia del Cimento
       </a>
       of Florence reported that it had performed Galileo's experiment, with the lanterns separated by about one mile, but no delay was observed. The actual delay in this experiment would have been about 11
       <a href="/wiki/Microsecond" title="Microsecond">
        microseconds
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_from_1676_article_on_Ole_R%C3%B8mer%27s_measurement_of_the_speed_of_light.jpg">
         <img alt="A diagram of a planet's orbit around the Sun and of a moon's orbit around another planet. The shadow of the latter planet is shaded." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="825" data-file-width="436" height="341" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Illustration_from_1676_article_on_Ole_R%C3%B8mer%27s_measurement_of_the_speed_of_light.jpg/180px-Illustration_from_1676_article_on_Ole_R%C3%B8mer%27s_measurement_of_the_speed_of_light.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Illustration_from_1676_article_on_Ole_R%C3%B8mer%27s_measurement_of_the_speed_of_light.jpg/270px-Illustration_from_1676_article_on_Ole_R%C3%B8mer%27s_measurement_of_the_speed_of_light.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Illustration_from_1676_article_on_Ole_R%C3%B8mer%27s_measurement_of_the_speed_of_light.jpg/360px-Illustration_from_1676_article_on_Ole_R%C3%B8mer%27s_measurement_of_the_speed_of_light.jpg 2x" width="180"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_from_1676_article_on_Ole_R%C3%B8mer%27s_measurement_of_the_speed_of_light.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Rømer's observations of the occultations of Io from Earth
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The first quantitative estimate of the speed of light was made in 1676 by Rømer (see
       <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B8mer%27s_determination_of_the_speed_of_light" title="Rømer's determination of the speed of light">
        Rømer's determination of the speed of light
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cohen_95-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-cohen-95">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-roemer_96-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-roemer-96">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       From the observation that the periods of Jupiter's innermost moon
       <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">
        Io
       </a>
       appeared to be shorter when the Earth was approaching Jupiter than when receding from it, he concluded that light travels at a finite speed, and estimated that it takes light 22 minutes to cross the diameter of Earth's orbit.
       <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">
        Christiaan Huygens
       </a>
       combined this estimate with an estimate for the diameter of the Earth's orbit to obtain an estimate of speed of light of
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008220000000000000♠
        </span>
        220
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         000
        </span>
        km/s
       </span>
       , 26% lower than the actual value.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huygens_1690_8–9_129-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Huygens_1690_8–9-129">
         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In his 1704 book
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Opticks" title="Opticks">
         Opticks
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">
        Isaac Newton
       </a>
       reported Rømer's calculations of the finite speed of light and gave a value of "seven or eight minutes" for the time taken for light to travel from the Sun to the Earth (the modern value is 8 minutes 19 seconds).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
        <a href="#cite_note-145">
         [133]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Newton queried whether Rømer's eclipse shadows were coloured; hearing that they were not, he concluded the different colours travelled at the same speed. In 1729,
       <a href="/wiki/James_Bradley" title="James Bradley">
        James Bradley
       </a>
       discovered
       <a href="/wiki/Aberration_of_light" title="Aberration of light">
        stellar aberration
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradley1729_97-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bradley1729-97">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       From this effect he determined that light must travel 10,210 times faster than the Earth in its orbit (the modern figure is 10,066 times faster) or, equivalently, that it would take light 8 minutes 12 seconds to travel from the Sun to the Earth.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bradley1729_97-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bradley1729-97">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Connections_with_electromagnetism">
        Connections with electromagnetism
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_electromagnetic_theory" title="History of electromagnetic theory">
        History of electromagnetic theory
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_special_relativity" title="History of special relativity">
        History of special relativity
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the 19th century
       <a href="/wiki/Hippolyte_Fizeau" title="Hippolyte Fizeau">
        Hippolyte Fizeau
       </a>
       developed a method to determine the speed of light based on time-of-flight measurements on Earth and reported a value of
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008315000000000000♠
        </span>
        315
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         000
        </span>
        km/s
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guarnieri_7-1_146-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-guarnieri_7-1-146">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
       His method was improved upon by
       <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Foucault" title="Léon Foucault">
        Léon Foucault
       </a>
       who obtained a value of
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008298000000000000♠
        </span>
        298
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         000
        </span>
        km/s
       </span>
       in 1862.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-How_108-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-How-108">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the year 1856,
       <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Eduard_Weber" title="Wilhelm Eduard Weber">
        Wilhelm Eduard Weber
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Kohlrausch" title="Rudolf Kohlrausch">
        Rudolf Kohlrausch
       </a>
       measured the ratio of the electromagnetic and electrostatic units of charge, 1/√
       <i>
        ε
       </i>
       <sub>
        0
       </sub>
       <i>
        μ
       </i>
       <sub>
        0
       </sub>
       , by discharging a
       <a href="/wiki/Leyden_jar" title="Leyden jar">
        Leyden jar
       </a>
       , and found that its numerical value was very close to the speed of light as measured directly by Fizeau. The following year
       <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Kirchhoff" title="Gustav Kirchhoff">
        Gustav Kirchhoff
       </a>
       calculated that an electric signal in a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_resistance" title="Electrical resistance">
        resistanceless
       </a>
       wire travels along the wire at this speed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
        <a href="#cite_note-147">
         [135]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the early 1860s, Maxwell showed that, according to the theory of electromagnetism he was working on, electromagnetic waves propagate in empty space
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
        <a href="#cite_note-148">
         [136]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
        <a href="#cite_note-149">
         [137]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
        <a href="#cite_note-150">
         [138]
        </a>
       </sup>
       at a speed equal to the above Weber/Kohrausch ratio, and drawing attention to the numerical proximity of this value to the speed of light as measured by Fizeau, he proposed that light is in fact an electromagnetic wave.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-maxwellbio_151-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-maxwellbio-151">
         [139]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id=".22Luminiferous_aether.22">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id='"Luminiferous_aether"'>
        "Luminiferous aether"
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Einstein_en_Lorentz.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2256" data-file-width="1712" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Einstein_en_Lorentz.jpg/170px-Einstein_en_Lorentz.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Einstein_en_Lorentz.jpg/255px-Einstein_en_Lorentz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Einstein_en_Lorentz.jpg/340px-Einstein_en_Lorentz.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Einstein_en_Lorentz.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Hendrik Lorentz (right) with Albert Einstein.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       It was thought at the time that empty space was filled with a background medium called the
       <a href="/wiki/Luminiferous_aether" title="Luminiferous aether">
        luminiferous aether
       </a>
       in which the electromagnetic field existed. Some physicists thought that this aether acted as a
       <a href="/wiki/Preferred_frame" title="Preferred frame">
        preferred frame
       </a>
       of reference for the propagation of light and therefore it should be possible to measure the motion of the Earth with respect to this medium, by measuring the isotropy of the speed of light. Beginning in the 1880s several experiments were performed to try to detect this motion, the most famous of which is
       <a href="/wiki/Michelson%E2%80%93Morley_experiment" title="Michelson–Morley experiment">
        the experiment
       </a>
       performed by
       <a href="/wiki/Albert_A._Michelson" title="Albert A. Michelson">
        Albert A. Michelson
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Edward_W._Morley" title="Edward W. Morley">
        Edward W. Morley
       </a>
       in 1887.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
        <a href="#cite_note-152">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The detected motion was always less than the observational error. Modern experiments indicate that the two-way speed of light is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">
        isotropic
       </a>
       (the same in every direction) to within 6 nanometres per second.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
        <a href="#cite_note-153">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Because of this experiment
       <a href="/wiki/Hendrik_Lorentz" title="Hendrik Lorentz">
        Hendrik Lorentz
       </a>
       proposed that the motion of the apparatus through the aether may cause the apparatus to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorentz_contraction" title="Lorentz contraction">
        contract
       </a>
       along its length in the direction of motion, and he further assumed, that the time variable for moving systems must also be changed accordingly ("local time"), which led to the formulation of the
       <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_transformation" title="Lorentz transformation">
        Lorentz transformation
       </a>
       . Based on
       <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_ether_theory" title="Lorentz ether theory">
        Lorentz's aether theory
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9" title="Henri Poincaré">
        Henri Poincaré
       </a>
       (1900) showed that this local time (to first order in v/c) is indicated by clocks moving in the aether, which are synchronized under the assumption of constant light speed. In 1904, he speculated that the speed of light could be a limiting velocity in dynamics, provided that the assumptions of Lorentz's theory are all confirmed. In 1905, Poincaré brought Lorentz's aether theory into full observational agreement with the
       <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_relativity" title="Principle of relativity">
        principle of relativity
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
        <a href="#cite_note-154">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
        <a href="#cite_note-155">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Special_relativity">
        Special relativity
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 1905 Einstein postulated from the outset that the speed of light in vacuum, measured by a non-accelerating observer, is independent of the motion of the source or observer. Using this and the principle of relativity as a basis he derived the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Special_theory_of_relativity" title="Special theory of relativity">
        special theory of relativity
       </a>
       , in which the speed of light in vacuum
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       featured as a fundamental constant, also appearing in contexts unrelated to light. This made the concept of the stationary aether (to which Lorentz and Poincaré still adhered) useless and revolutionized the concepts of space and time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
        <a href="#cite_note-156">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
        <a href="#cite_note-157">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Increased_accuracy_of_c_and_redefinition_of_the_metre_and_second">
        Increased accuracy of
        <i>
         c
        </i>
        and redefinition of the metre and second
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_metre" title="History of the metre">
        History of the metre
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the second half of the 20th century much progress was made in increasing the accuracy of measurements of the speed of light, first by cavity resonance techniques and later by laser interferometer techniques. These were aided by new, more precise, definitions of the metre and second. In 1950,
       <a href="/wiki/Louis_Essen" title="Louis Essen">
        Louis Essen
       </a>
       determined the speed as
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008299792500000000♠
        </span>
        299
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         792
        </span>
        .5
        <span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ±
        </span>
        1 km/s
       </span>
       , using cavity resonance. This value was adopted by the 12th General Assembly of the Radio-Scientific Union in 1957. In 1960, the
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_metre#Krypton_standard" title="History of the metre">
        metre was redefined
       </a>
       in terms of the wavelength of a particular spectral line of krypton-86, and, in 1967, the
       <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">
        second
       </a>
       was redefined in terms of the hyperfine transition frequency of the ground state of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesium-133" title="Caesium-133">
        caesium-133
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1972, using the laser interferometer method and the new definitions, a group at the US
       <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">
        National Bureau of Standards
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Boulder,_Colorado" title="Boulder, Colorado">
        Boulder, Colorado
       </a>
       determined the speed of light in vacuum to be
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       =
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008299792456200000♠
        </span>
        299
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         792
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         456
        </span>
        .2
        <span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">
         ±
        </span>
        1.1 m/s
       </span>
       . This was 100 times less
       <a href="/wiki/Measurement_uncertainty" title="Measurement uncertainty">
        uncertain
       </a>
       than the previously accepted value. The remaining uncertainty was mainly related to the definition of the metre.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
        <a href="#cite_note-160">
         [Note 13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIST_heterodyne_123-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-NIST_heterodyne-123">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As similar experiments found comparable results for
       <i>
        c
       </i>
       , the 15th
       <a href="/wiki/General_Conference_on_Weights_and_Measures" title="General Conference on Weights and Measures">
        General Conference on Weights and Measures
       </a>
       in 1975 recommended using the value
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008299792458000000♠
        </span>
        299
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         792
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         458
        </span>
        m/s
       </span>
       for the speed of light.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15thCGPM_161-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-15thCGPM-161">
         [148]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Defining_the_speed_of_light_as_an_explicit_constant">
        Defining the speed of light as an explicit constant
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In 1983 the 17th CGPM found that wavelengths from frequency measurements and a given value for the speed of light are more
       <a href="/wiki/Reproducibility" title="Reproducibility">
        reproducible
       </a>
       than the previous standard. They kept the 1967 definition of
       <a href="/wiki/Second" title="Second">
        second
       </a>
       , so the
       <a href="/wiki/Caesium" title="Caesium">
        caesium
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Hyperfine_structure#Use_in_defining_the_SI_second_and_meter" title="Hyperfine structure">
        hyperfine frequency
       </a>
       would now determine both the second and the metre. To do this, they redefined the metre as: "The metre is the length of the path travelled by light in vacuum during a time interval of 1/
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008299792458000000♠
        </span>
        299
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         792
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         458
        </span>
       </span>
       of a second."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Resolution_1_94-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Resolution_1-94">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As a result of this definition, the value of the speed of light in vacuum is exactly
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008299792458000000♠
        </span>
        299
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         792
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         458
        </span>
        m/s
       </span>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheeler_162-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Wheeler-162">
         [149]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_163-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-timeline-163">
         [150]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and has become a defined constant in the SI system of units.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jespersen_15-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jespersen-15">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Improved experimental techniques that prior to 1983 would have measured the speed of light, no longer affect the known value of the speed of light in SI units, but instead allow a more precise realization of the metre by more accurately measuring the wavelength of Krypton-86 and other light sources.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adams_164-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Adams-164">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W_Rindler_165-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-W_Rindler-165">
         [152]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2011, the CGPM stated its intention to redefine all seven SI base units using what it calls "the explicit-constant formulation", where each "unit is defined indirectly by specifying explicitly an exact value for a well-recognized fundamental constant", as was done for the speed of light. It proposed a new, but completely equivalent, wording of the metre's definition: "The metre, symbol m, is the unit of length; its magnitude is set by fixing the numerical value of the speed of light in vacuum to be equal to exactly
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7008299792458000000♠
        </span>
        299
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         792
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         458
        </span>
       </span>
       when it is expressed in the SI unit
       <span class="nowrap">
        m s
        <sup>
         −1
        </sup>
       </span>
       ."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
        <a href="#cite_note-166">
         [153]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is one of the proposed changes to be incorporated in the
       <a href="/wiki/Proposed_redefinition_of_SI_base_units" title="Proposed redefinition of SI base units">
        next revision of the SI
       </a>
       also termed the
       <i>
        New SI
       </i>
       .
      </p>

         <span class="reference-text">

          <a href="/wiki/Imperial_units" title="Imperial units">
           imperial units
          </a>
          and
          <a href="/wiki/United_States_customary_units" title="United States customary units">

          is based on an inch of exactly
          <span class="nowrap">
           <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">

          </span>
          and is exactly 186,282 miles, 698 yards, 2 feet, and
          <span class="sfrac nowrap">

           <span class="visualhide">
           </span>

            <span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">

            </span>

            </span>

          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
           <a href="#cite_note-16">
            [13]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-19">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

           <a href="#cite_ref-19">

           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          However, the
          <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">
           frequency
          </a>
          of light can depend on the motion of the source relative to the observer, due to the
          <a href="/wiki/Doppler_effect" title="Doppler effect">
           Doppler effect
          </a>
          .
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-28">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-28">

           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Whereas moving objects are
          <i>
           measured
          </i>
          to be shorter along the line of relative motion, they are also
          <i>
           seen
          </i>
          as being rotated. This effect, known as
          <a href="/wiki/Terrell_rotation" title="Terrell rotation">
           Terrell rotation
          </a>
          , is due to the different times that light from different parts of the object takes to reach the observer.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
           <a href="#cite_note-26">
            [22]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
           <a href="#cite_note-27">
            [23]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-32">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-32">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          The interpretation of observations on binary systems used to determine the speed of gravity is considered doubtful by some authors, leaving the experimental situation uncertain.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brügmann_31-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-Brügmann-31">
            [26]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-45">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-45">

           </a>

         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          It is thought that the
          <a href="/wiki/Scharnhorst_effect" title="Scharnhorst effect">
           Scharnhorst effect
          </a>
          does allow signals to travel slightly faster than
          <i>
           c
          </i>
          , but the special conditions in which this effect can occur prevent one from using this effect to violate causality.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
           <a href="#cite_note-44">
            [38]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-83">

           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          A typical value for the refractive index of optical fibre is between 1.518 and 1.538.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Midwinter_82-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-Midwinter-82">
            [75]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-102">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-102">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          The astronomical unit was defined as the radius of an unperturbed circular Newtonian orbit about the Sun of a particle having infinitesimal mass, moving with an
          <a href="/wiki/Angular_frequency" title="Angular frequency">
           angular frequency
          </a>
          of
          <span style="white-space:nowrap">
           0.017
           <span style="margin-left:0.25em">
            202
           </span>
           <span style="margin-left:0.25em">
            098
           </span>
           <span style="margin-left:0.25em">
            95
           </span>
          </span>
          <a href="/wiki/Radian" title="Radian">
           radians
          </a>
          (approximately ​
          <span class="frac nowrap">
           <sup>
            1
           </sup>
           ⁄
           <sub>
            <span class="nowrap">
             <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
              7002365256898000000♠
             </span>
             365.256
             <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
              898
             </span>
            </span>
           </sub>
          </span>
          of a revolution) per day.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
           <a href="#cite_note-101">
            [93]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-107">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-107">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Nevertheless, at this degree of precision, the effects of
          <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">
           general relativity
          </a>
          must be taken into consideration when interpreting the length. The metre is considered to be a unit of
          <a href="/wiki/Proper_length" title="Proper length">
           proper length
          </a>
          , whereas the AU is usually used as a unit of observed length in a given frame of reference. The values cited here follow the latter convention, and are
          <a href="/wiki/Barycentric_Dynamical_Time" title="Barycentric Dynamical Time">
           TDB
          </a>
          -compatible.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU_104-1">
           <a href="#cite_note-IAU-104">
            [95]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-120">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-120">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          A detailed discussion of the interferometer and its use for determining the speed of light can be found in Vaughan (1989).
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaughan_119-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-Vaughan-119">
            [109]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-127">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-127">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          According to Galileo, the lanterns he used were "at a short distance, less than a mile." Assuming the distance was not too much shorter than a mile, and that "about a thirtieth of a second is the minimum time interval distinguishable by the unaided eye", Boyer notes that Galileo's experiment could at best be said to have established a lower limit of about 60 miles per second for the velocity of light.
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-160">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-160">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          Between 1960 and 1983 the metre was defined as: "The metre is the length equal to
          <span class="nowrap">
           <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
            7006165076373000000♠
           </span>
           1
           <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
            650
           </span>
           <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
            763
           </span>
           .73
          </span>
          wavelengths in vacuum of the radiation corresponding to the transition between the levels 2p
          <sup>
           10
          </sup>
          and 5d
          <sup>
           5
          </sup>
          of the krypton 86 atom."
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11thCGPM_158-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-11thCGPM-158">
            [146]
           </a>
          </sup>
          It was discovered in the 1970s that this spectral line was not symmetric, which put a limit on the precision with which the definition could be realized in interferometry experiments.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
           <a href="#cite_note-159">
            [147]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </span>
        </li>
       </ol>

          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Elementary+and+Intermediate+Algebra%3A+A+Combined+Course%2C+Student+Support+Edition&amp;rft.pages=197&amp;rft.edition=4th+illudtrated&amp;rft.pub=Cengage+Learning&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-618-75354-3&amp;rft.aulast=Larson&amp;rft.aufirst=Ron&amp;rft.au=Hostetler%2C+Robert+P.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3Dqe-YvKoeiasC%26pg%3DPA179&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASpeed+of+light">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-penrose-5">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-penrose_5-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>

         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation book">
           Penrose, R (2004).
           <i>
            <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Road_to_Reality:_A_Complete_Guide_to_the_Laws_of_the_Universe" title="The Road to Reality: A Complete Guide to the Laws of the Universe">
             The Road to Reality: A Complete Guide to the Laws of the Universe
            </a>
           </i>
           .
           <a href="/wiki/Vintage_Books" title="Vintage Books">
            Vintage Books
           </a>
           . pp. 410–11.
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
            ISBN
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-679-77631-4" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-679-77631-4">
            978-0-679-77631-4
           </a>
           .
           <q>
            ... the most accurate standard for the metre is conveniently
            <i>
             defined
            </i>
            so that there are exactly 299,792,458 of them to the distance travelled by light in a standard second, giving a value for the metre that very accurately matches the now inadequately precise standard metre rule in Paris.
           </q>
