        <li id="cite_note-Yc-10">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-Yc_10-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation web">
           Gibbs, P (2004) [1997].
           <a class="external text" href="https://www.webcitation.org/5lLMPPN4L?url=http://math.ucr.edu/home/baez/physics/Relativity/SpeedOfLight/c.html" rel="nofollow">
            "Why is
            <i>
             c
            </i>
            the symbol for the speed of light?"
           </a>
           .
           <i>
            Usenet Physics FAQ
           </i>
           .
           <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Riverside" title="University of California, Riverside">
            University of California, Riverside
           </a>
           . Archived from
           <a class="external text" href="http://math.ucr.edu/home/baez/physics/Relativity/SpeedOfLight/c.html" rel="nofollow">
            the original
           </a>
           on 2009-11-17
           <span class="reference-accessdate">
            . Retrieved
            <span class="nowrap">
             2009-11-16
            </span>
           </span>
           .
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Usenet+Physics+FAQ&amp;rft.atitle=Why+is+c+the+symbol+for+the+speed+of+light%3F&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.aulast=Gibbs&amp;rft.aufirst=P&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fmath.ucr.edu%2Fhome%2Fbaez%2Fphysics%2FRelativity%2FSpeedOfLight%2Fc.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASpeed+of+light">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
          "The origins of the letter c being used for the speed of light can be traced back to a paper of 1856 by Weber and Kohlrausch [...] Weber apparently meant c to stand for 'constant' in his force law, but there is evidence that physicists such as Lorentz and Einstein were accustomed to a common convention that c could be used as a variable for velocity. This usage can be traced back to the classic Latin texts in which c stood for 'celeritas' meaning 'speed'."
         </span>
        </li>

        <li id="cite_note-Boyes-13">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-Boyes_13-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>

         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation book">
           Sydenham, PH (2003).
           <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=sarHIbCVOUAC&amp;pg=PA56" rel="nofollow">
            "Measurement of length"
           </a>
           . In Boyes, W.
           <i>
            Instrumentation Reference Book
           </i>
           (3rd ed.).
           <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Butterworth%E2%80%93Heinemann" title="Butterworth–Heinemann">
            Butterworth–Heinemann
           </a>
           . p. 56.
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
            ISBN
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-7506-7123-8" title="Special:BookSources/0-7506-7123-8">
            0-7506-7123-8
           </a>
           .
           <q>
            ... if the speed of light is defined as a fixed number then, in principle, the time standard will serve as the length standard ...
           </q>

           <span style="padding-left:0.2em;">
            '
           </span>

           <q>
            The possibility that the fundamental constants may vary during the evolution of the universe offers an exceptional window onto higher dimensional theories and is probably linked with the nature of the dark energy that makes the universe accelerate today.
           </q>

          </cite>

        <li id="cite_note-Adams-164">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-Adams_164-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation book">
           Adams, S (1997).
           <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/?id=1RV0AysEN4oC&amp;pg=PA140" rel="nofollow">
            <i>
             Relativity: An Introduction to Space–Time Physics
            </i>
           </a>
           . CRC Press. p. 140.
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
            ISBN
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-7484-0621-2" title="Special:BookSources/0-7484-0621-2">
            0-7484-0621-2
           </a>
           .
           <q>
            One peculiar consequence of this system of definitions is that any future refinement in our ability to measure
            <i>
             c
            </i>
            will not change the speed of light (which is a defined number), but will change the length of the meter!
           </q>
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Relativity%3A+An+Introduction+to+Space%E2%80%93Time+Physics&amp;rft.pages=140&amp;rft.pub=CRC+Press&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.isbn=0-7484-0621-2&amp;rft.aulast=Adams&amp;rft.aufirst=S&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3D1RV0AysEN4oC%26pg%3DPA140&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASpeed+of+light">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-W_Rindler-165">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-W_Rindler_165-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <cite class="citation book">
           Rindler, W (2006).
           <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/?id=MuuaG5HXOGEC&amp;pg=PT41" rel="nofollow">
            <i>
             Relativity: Special, General, and Cosmological
            </i>
           </a>
           (2nd ed.).
           <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Press" title="Oxford University Press">
            Oxford University Press
           </a>
           . p. 41.
           <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
            ISBN
           </a>
           <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-19-856731-6" title="Special:BookSources/0-19-856731-6">
            0-19-856731-6
           </a>
           .
           <q>
            Note that [...] improvements in experimental accuracy will modify the meter relative to atomic wavelengths, but not the value of the speed of light!
           </q>
          </cite>
          <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Relativity%3A+Special%2C+General%2C+and+Cosmological&amp;rft.pages=41&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.isbn=0-19-856731-6&amp;rft.aulast=Rindler&amp;rft.aufirst=W&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2F%3Fid%3DMuuaG5HXOGEC%26pg%3DPT41&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASpeed+of+light">
           <span style="display:none;">
           </span>
          </span>
         </span>
        </li>
        <li id="cite_note-166">
